Preface
Noncommutative geometry is the study of noncommutative algebras as if they were
algebras of functions on spaces, like the commutative algebras associated to aï¬ƒne
algebraic varieties, diï¬€erentiable manifolds, topological spaces, and measure spaces.
In this book, we discuss several types of geometric objects (in the usual sense of
sets with structure) which are closely related to noncommutative algebras.
Central to the discussion are symplectic and Poisson manifolds, which arise
when noncommutative algebras are obtained by deforming commutative algebras.
We also make a detailed study of groupoids, whose role in noncommutative geom-
etry has been stressed by Connes, as well as of Lie algebroids, the inï¬nitesimal
approximations to diï¬€erentiable groupoids.
These notes are based on a topics course, â€œGeometric Models for Noncommuta-
tive Algebras,â€ which one of us (A.W.) taught at Berkeley in the Spring of 1997.
We would like to express our appreciation to Kevin Hartshorn for his partic-
ipation in the early stages of the project â€“ producing typed notes for many of
the lectures. Henrique Bursztyn, who read preliminary versions of the notes, has
provided us with innumerable suggestions of great value. We are also indebted
to Johannes Huebschmann, Kirill Mackenzie, Daniel Markiewicz, Elisa Prato and
Olga Radko for several useful commentaries or references.
Finally, we would like to dedicate these notes to the memory of four friends and
colleagues who, sadly, passed away in 1998: MoshÂ´e Flato, K. Guruprasad, AndrÂ´e
Lichnerowicz, and Stanis law Zakrzewski.
Ana Cannas da Silva
Alan Weinstein
xi

Introduction
We will emphasize an approach to algebra and geometry based on a metaphor (see
Lakoï¬€and NuËœnez [100]):
An algebra (over R or C) is the set of (R- or C-valued) functions on a space.
Strictly speaking, this statement only holds for commutative algebras. We would
like to pretend that this statement still describes noncommutative algebras.
Furthermore, diï¬€erent restrictions on the functions reveal diï¬€erent structures
on the space. Examples of distinct algebras of functions which can be associated
to a space are:
â€¢ polynomial functions,
â€¢ real analytic functions,
â€¢ smooth functions,
â€¢ Ck, or just continuous (C0) functions,
â€¢ Lâˆ, or the set of bounded, measurable functions modulo the set of functions
vanishing outside a set of measure 0.
So we can actually say,
An algebra (over R or C) is the set of good (R- or C-valued) functions on a space
with structure.
Reciprocally, we would like to be able to recover the space with structure from
the given algebra. In algebraic geometry that is achieved by considering homomor-
phisms from the algebra to a ï¬eld or integral domain.
Examples.
1. Take the algebra C[x] of complex polynomials in one complex variable. All
homomorphisms from C[x] to C are given by evaluation at a complex number.
We recover C as the space of homomorphisms.
2. Take the quotient algebra of C[x] by the ideal generated by xk+1

function plus its ï¬rst, second, ..., kth derivatives at the origin. The corre-
sponding â€œspaceâ€ is the so-called kth inï¬nitesimal neighborhood of the
point 0. Each of these â€œspacesâ€ has just one point: evaluation at 0. The limit
as k gets large is the space of power series in x.

5. The algebra â„¦â€¢(M) of diï¬€erential forms on a manifold M can be regarded as
a Z2-graded algebra by

We may thus think of forms on M as functions on a superspace. Locally, the
tangent bundle TM has coordinates {xi} and {dxi}, where each xi commutes

The exterior derivative

has the property that for f, g âˆˆâ„¦â€¢(M),

Hence, d is a derivation of a superalgebra. It exchanges the subspaces of even
and odd degree. We call d an odd vector ï¬eld on â—¦TM.
6. Consider the algebra of complex valued functions on a â€œphase spaceâ€ R2,
with coordinates (q, p) interpreted as position and momentum for a one-
dimensional physical system. We wish to impose the standard equation from
quantum mechanics
qp âˆ’pq = iâ„,

INTRODUCTION
which encodes the uncertainty principle. In order to formalize this condition,
we take the algebra freely generated by q and p modulo the ideal generated by
qpâˆ’pq âˆ’iâ„. As â„approaches 0, we recover the commutative algebra Pol(R2).
Studying examples like this naturally leads us toward the universal envelop-
ing algebra of a Lie algebra (here the Lie algebra is the Heisenberg algebra,
where â„is considered as a variable like q and p), and towards symplectic
geometry (here we concentrate on the phase space with coordinates q and
p).
â™¦
Each of these latter aspects will lead us into the study of Poisson algebras,
and the interplay between Poisson geometry and noncommutative algebras, in par-
ticular, connections with representation theory and operator algebras.
In these notes we will be also looking at groupoids, Lie groupoids and groupoid
algebras. Brieï¬‚y, a groupoid is similar to a group, but we can only multiply certain
pairs of elements. One can think of a groupoid as a category (possibly with more
than one object) where all morphisms are invertible, whereas a group is a category
with only one object such that all morphisms have inverses. Lie algebroids are
the inï¬nitesimal counterparts of Lie groupoids, and are very close to Poisson and
symplectic geometry.
Finally, we will discuss Fedosovâ€™s work in deformation quantization of arbitrary
symplectic manifolds.
All of these topics give nice geometric models for noncommutative algebras!
Of course, we could go on, but we had to stop somewhere. In particular, these
notes contain almost no discussion of Poisson Lie groups or symplectic groupoids,
both of which are special cases of Poisson groupoids. Ample material on Poisson
groups can be found in [25], while symplectic groupoids are discussed in [162] as
well as the original sources [34, 89, 181]. The theory of Poisson groupoids [168] is
evolving rapidly thanks to new examples found in conjunction with solutions of the
classical dynamical Yang-Baxter equation [136].
The time should not be long before a sequel to these notes is due.

Part I
Universal Enveloping Algebras
1
Algebraic Constructions
Let g be a Lie algebra with Lie bracket [Â·, Â·]. We will assume that g is a ï¬nite
dimensional algebra over R or C, but much of the following also holds for inï¬nite
dimensional Lie algebras, as well as for Lie algebras over arbitrary ï¬elds or rings.
1.1
Universal Enveloping Algebras

Remark.
The Leibniz identity says that {Â·, w} is a derivation of the associative
algebra structure; it is a compatibility property between the Lie algebra and the
associative algebra structures. Similarly, the Jacobi identity says that {Â·, w} is a
derivation of the Lie algebra structure.

certain diï¬€erential-geometric structure on an underlying space.

2.3
The Role of the Jacobi Identity
Choose a basis v1, . . . , vn for g. Let j : g ,â†’T (g) be the inclusion map. The algebra
T (g) is linearly generated by all monomials
j(vÎ±1) âŠ—. . . âŠ—j(vÎ±k) .
If i : g â†’U(g) is the natural map (as in Section 1.1), it is easy to see, via the
relation i(x) âŠ—i(y) âˆ’i(y) âŠ—i(x) = i([x, y]) in U(g), that the universal enveloping
algebra is generated by monomials of the form
i(vÎ±1) âŠ—. . . âŠ—i(vÎ±k) ,
Î±1 â‰¤. . . â‰¤Î±k .
However, it is not as trivial to show that there are no linear relations between these
generating monomials. Any proof of the independence of these generators must use
the Jacobi identity. The Jacobi identity is crucial since U(g) was deï¬ned to be an
universal object relative to the category of Lie algebras.
Forget for a moment about the Jacobi identity.
We deï¬ne an almost Lie
algebra g to be the same as a Lie algebra except that the bracket operation does not
necessarily satisfy the Jacobi identity. It is not diï¬ƒcult to see that the constructions
for the universal enveloping algebra still hold true in this category. We will test the
independence of the generating monomials of U(g) in this case. Let x, y, z âˆˆg for
some almost Lie algebra g. The jacobiator is the trilinear map J : g Ã— g Ã— g â†’g
deï¬ned by
J(x, y, z) = [x, [y, z]] + [y, [z, x]] + [z, [x, y]] .
Clearly, on a Lie algebra, the jacobiator vanishes; in general, it measures the ob-
struction to the Jacobi identity. Since J is antisymmetric in the three entries, we
can view it as a map g âˆ§g âˆ§g â†’g, which we will still denote by J.
Claim.
i : g â†’U(g) vanishes on the image of J.
This implies that we need J â‰¡0 for i to be an injection and the PoincarÂ´e-
Birkhoï¬€-Witt theorem to hold.
Proof.
Take x, y, z âˆˆg, and look at
i (J(x, y, z)) = i ([[x, y, ], z] + c.p.) .
Here, c.p. indicates that the succeeding terms are given by applying circular per-
mutations to the x, y, z of the ï¬rst term. Because i is linear and commutes with
the bracket operation, we see that
i (J(x, y, z)) = [[i(x), i(y)]U(g), i(z)]U(g) + c.p. .
But the bracket in the associative algebra always satisï¬es the Jacobi identity, and
so i(J) â‰¡0.

2.4
Actions of Lie Algebras
Much of this section traces back to the work of Lie around the end of the 19th
century on the existence of a Lie group G whose Lie algebra is a given Lie algebra
g.
Our proof of the PoincarÂ´e-Birkhoï¬€-Witt theorem will only require local existence
of G â€“ a neighborhood of the identity element in the group. What we shall construct
is a manifold M with a Lie algebra homomorphism from g to vector ï¬elds on M,
Ï : g â†’Ï‡(M), such that a basis of vectors on g goes to a pointwise linearly
independent set of vector ï¬elds on M. Such a map Ï is called a pointwise faithful
representation, or free action of g on M.
Example.
Let M = G be a Lie group with Lie algebra g.
Then the map
taking elements of g to left invariant vector ï¬elds on G (the generators of the right
translations) is a free action.
â™¦
The Lie algebra homomorphism Ï : g â†’Ï‡(M) is called a right action of the
Lie algebra g on M. (For left actions, Ï would have to be an anti-homomorphism.)
Such actions Ï can be obtained by diï¬€erentiating right actions of the Lie group G.
One of Lieâ€™s theorems shows that any homomorphism Ï can be integrated to a local
action of the group G on M.
Let v1, . . . , vn be a basis of g, and V1 = Ï(v1), . . . , Vn = Ï(vn) the corresponding
vector ï¬elds on M. Assume that the Vj are pointwise linearly independent. Since
Ï is a Lie algebra homomorphism, we have relations
[Vi, Vj] =

by the relations [vi, vj] = P cijkvk. In other words, {V1, . . . , Vn} is a set of vector

These

of TM. By the Frobenius theorem, we can integrate it. Let N âŠ†M be a leaf of the
corresponding foliation. There is a map ÏN : g â†’Ï‡(N) such that the Vj = ÏN(vj)â€™s

Although we will not need this fact for the PoincarÂ´e-Birkhoï¬€-Witt theorem,
we note that the leaf N is, in a sense, locally the Lie group with Lie algebra g:
Pick some point in N and label it e. There is a unique local group structure on
a neighborhood of e such that e is the identity element and V1, . . . , Vn are left
invariant vector ï¬elds. The group structure comes from deï¬ning the ï¬‚ows of the
vector ï¬elds to be right translations. The hard part of this construction is showing
that the multiplication deï¬ned in this way is associative.

Part II
Poisson Geometry
3
Poisson Structures
Let g be a ï¬nite dimensional Lie algebra with Lie bracket [Â·, Â·]g. In Section 2.2, we
deï¬ned a Poisson bracket {Â·, Â·} on Gr U(g) using the commutator bracket in U(g)

induces a Poisson bracket on Pol(gâˆ—).

A pair (M, {Â·, Â·}) is called an almost Poisson manifold when {Â·, Â·} is an almost

3.3
Poisson Manifolds
An almost Poisson structure {Â·, Â·}Î  on a manifold M is called a Poisson structure
if it satisï¬es the Jacobi identity. A Poisson manifold (M, {Â·, Â·}) is a manifold M
equipped with a Poisson structure {Â·, Â·}. The corresponding bivector ï¬eld Î  is then
called a Poisson tensor. The name â€œPoisson structureâ€ sometimes refers to the
bracket {Â·, Â·} and sometimes to the Poisson tensor Î .

2
Historical Remark.
This lemma gives another formulation of the integrability
condition for Î , which, in fact, was the original version of the identity as formulated
by Jacobi around 1838.
(See Jacobiâ€™s collected works [86].)
Poisson [138] had
introduced the bracket {Â·, Â·} in order to simplify calculations in celestial mechanics.
He proved around 1808, through long and tedious computations, that
{f, h} = 0
and
{g, h} = 0
=â‡’
{{f, g}, h} = 0 .
This means that, if two functions f, g are constant along integral curves of Xh,
then one can form a third function also constant along Xh, namely {f, g}. When
Jacobi later stated the identity in Lemma 3.1, he gave a much shorter proof of a
yet stronger result.

4
Normal Forms
Throughout this and the next chapter, our goal is to understand what Poisson
manifolds look like geometrically.
4.1
Lieâ€™s Normal Form
We will prove the following result in Section 4.3.
Theorem 4.1 (Lie [106]) If Î  is a Poisson structure on M whose matrix of
structure functions, Ï€ij(x), has constant rank, then each point of M is contained
in a local coordinate system with respect to which (Ï€ij) is constant.
Remarks.
1. The assumption above of constant rank was not stated by Lie, although it
was used implicitly in his proof.
2. Since Theorem 4.1 is a local result, we only need to require the matrix (Ï€ij)
to have locally constant rank. This is a reasonable condition to impose, as
the structure functions Ï€ij will always have locally constant rank on an open
dense set of M. To see this, notice that the set of points in M where (Ï€ij)
has maximal rank is open, and then proceed inductively on the complement
of the closure of this set (exercise!). Notice that the set of points where the
rank of (Ï€ij) is maximal is not necessarily dense. For instance, consider R2
with {x1, x2} = Ï•(x1, x2) given by an arbitrary function Ï•.
3. Points where (Ï€ij) has locally constant rank are called regular. If all points
of M are regular, M is called a regular Poisson manifold. A Lie-Poisson
manifold gâˆ—is not regular unless g is abelian, though the regular points of gâˆ—
form, of course, an open dense subset.

5
Local Poisson Geometry
Roughly speaking, any Poisson manifold is obtained by gluing together symplectic
manifolds. The study of Poisson structures involves both local and global concerns:
the local structure of symplectic leaves and their transverse structures, and the
global aspects of how symplectic leaves ï¬t together into a foliation.
5.1
Symplectic Foliation
At a regular point p of a Poisson manifold M, the subspace of TpM spanned by the
hamiltonian vector ï¬elds of the canonical coordinates at that point depends only
on the Poisson structure. When the Poisson structure is regular (see Section 4.1),
the image of eÎ  (formed by the subspaces above) is an involutive subbundle of TM.
Hence, there is a natural foliation of M by symplectic manifolds whose dimension
is the rank of Î . These are called the symplectic leaves, forming the symplectic
foliation.
It is a remarkable fact that symplectic leaves exist through every point, even
on Poisson manifolds (M, {Â·, Â·}) where the Poisson structure is not regular. (Their
existence was ï¬rst proved in this context by Kirillov [95].) In general, the symplectic
foliation is a singular foliation.
The symplectic leaves are determined locally by the splitting theorem (Sec-
tion 4.3).
For any point O of the Poisson manifold, if (q, p, y) are the normal
coordinates as in Theorem 4.2, then the symplectic leaf through O is given locally
by the equation y = 0.
The Poisson brackets on M can be calculated by restricting to the symplectic
leaves and then assembling the results.

to think of H1

5.3
The Linearization Problem
Suppose that we have structure functions
Ï€ij(y) =

cijkyk + O(y2) .

This question resembles Morse theory where, given a function whose Taylor
expansion only has quadratic terms or higher, we ask whether there exist some
coordinates for which the higher terms vanish. The answer is yes (without further
assumptions on the function) if and only if the quadratic part is non-degenerate.
When the answer to the linearization problem is aï¬ƒrmative, we call the structure
Ï€ij linearizable. Given ï¬xed cijk, if Ï€ij is linearizable for all choices of O(y2),

of some results on the non-degeneracy of Lie algebras.
â€¢ It is not hard to see that the zero (or commutative) Lie algebra is degenerate

â€¢ Cahen, Gutt and Rawnsley [22] studied the non-linearizability of some Poisson

Poisson Category
6
Poisson Maps
Any Poisson manifold has an associated Poisson algebra, namely the algebra of
its smooth functions equipped with the Poisson bracket. In this chapter, we will
strengthen the analogy between algebras and spaces.
6.1

Given two Poisson algebras A, B, an algebra homomorphism Ïˆ : A â†’B is called a
Poisson-algebra homomorphism if Ïˆ preserves Poisson brackets:

of M which is a Poisson map.

6.2
Complete Poisson Maps
Although a Poisson map Ï• : M â†’N preserves brackets, the image is not in general
a union of symplectic leaves. Here is why: For a point x âˆˆM, the image Ï•(x) lies
on some symplectic leaf O in N. We can reach any other point y âˆˆO from Ï•(x) by
following the trajectory of (possibly more than one) hamiltonian vector ï¬eld Xh.
While we can lift Xh to the hamiltonian vector ï¬eld XÏ•âˆ—h near x, knowing that
Xh is complete does not ensure that XÏ•âˆ—h is complete. Consequently, we may not
be able to lift the entire trajectory of Xh, so the point y is not necessarily in the
image of Ï•. Still, the image of Ï• is a union of open subsets of symplectic leaves.
The following example provides a trivial illustration of this fact.

To exclude examples like this we make the following deï¬nition.
A Poisson map Ï• : M â†’N is complete if, for each h âˆˆCâˆ(N), Xh being a
complete vector ï¬eld implies that XÏ•âˆ—h is also complete.
Proposition 6.1 The image of a complete Poisson map is a union of symplectic
leaves.
Proof.
From any image point Ï•(x), we can reach any other point on the same
symplectic leaf of N by a chain of integral curves of complete hamiltonian vector

the XÏ•âˆ—hâ€™s are also complete. Hence, we can integrate them without restriction,

Remarks.
1. In the deï¬nition of complete map, we can replace completeness of Xh by the
condition that Xh has compact support, or even by the condition that h has
compact support.
2. A Poisson map does not necessarily map symplectic leaves into symplectic
leaves. Even in the simple example (previous section) of projection R2n â†’Rn,
while R2n has only one leaf, each point of Rn is a symplectic leaf.
â™¦
The example of projecting R2n to Rn is important to keep in mind. This projec-
tion is a complete Poisson map, as Xh is always trivial (and thus complete) on Rn
and the pull-back âˆ’P âˆ‚h

projection to a subset of R2n, then the map will in general no longer be complete.
The subsets of R2n for which the projection restricts to a complete map are those
which are open collections of full vertical p-ï¬bers.
Here is another justiï¬cation of our terminology.

6.3
Symplectic Realizations
A Poisson map Ï• : M â†’N from a symplectic manifold M is called a symplectic
realization of the Poisson manifold N.
Examples.
1. A basic example of symplectic realization is the inclusion map of a symplectic
leaf into the ambient Poisson manifold.

6.3
Symplectic Realizations
33
2. A more signiï¬cant example is provided by our construction in Section 4.2 of a
faithful representation of g. We took an open subset U of gâˆ—with coordinates
(q, p, c) and formed the symplectic space U Ã— Râ„“with coordinates (q, p, c, d).
The map projecting U Ã— Râ„“back to gâˆ—is a symplectic realization for gâˆ—.
It is certainly not a complete Poisson map. It was constructed to have the
property that functions on gâˆ—with linearly independent diï¬€erentials pull back
to functions on U Ã— Râ„“with linearly independent hamiltonian vector ï¬elds.

If a symplectic realization Ï• : M â†’N is a submersion, then locally there is
a faithful representation of the functions on N (modulo the constants) by vector

quite general:
Theorem 6.3 (Karasev [89], Weinstein [34]) Every Poisson manifold has a
surjective submersive symplectic realization.
The proof of this theorem (which is omitted here) relies on ï¬nding symplectic
realizations of open subsets covering a Poisson manifold and patching them together
using a uniqueness property.
It is often diï¬ƒcult to ï¬nd the realization explicitly.
We do not know whether completeness can be required in this theorem.
Example.

6.5

There is some terminology commonly used in these constructions. An action of

actions are simply referred to as hamiltonian while hamiltonian actions as we have
deï¬ned them are called strongly hamiltonian.

In this chapter, we introduce terminology and quote results leading to the double

Morita Equivalence
Let J1, J2 be surjective Poisson submersions from a symplectic manifold M to

The reverse implication is not true unless we assume that the ï¬bers are connected,

was a global condition. If J1 and J2 have connected ï¬bers, then the two conditions

the following property for such dual pairs.
Proposition 9.2 Let J1, J2 be a pair of complete surjective Poisson submersions

P1

from a symplectic manifold M. Assume that the J1-ï¬bers are symplectically orthog-

correspondence between the symplectic leaves of P1 and the symplectic leaves of P2.
Proof.
Let Fj âŠ†TM be the distribution spanned by the hamiltonian vector
ï¬elds of functions in Jâˆ—

distribution F1 (respectively F2) gives the subspace tangent to the ï¬bers of J2
(respectively J1); this clearly shows that each of F1 and F2 is integrable. To see
that F1 + F2 is also integrable, note that F1 + F2 is spanned by hamiltonian vector
ï¬elds, and that the vector ï¬elds from J1 commute with those from J2. So we can
integrate F1 + F2 to a foliation of M.
A leaf L of the foliation deï¬ned by F1 + F2 projects by each map Ji to a set
Ji(L), which is in fact a symplectic leaf of Pi (i = 1, 2) for the following two reasons.

9.5
Representation Equivalence
55
First, by completeness, we can move anywhere within a symplectic leaf of Pi by
moving in the Fi direction in L. Secondly, if we move in the F2 (respectively F1)
direction in L, then nothing happens in the projection to P1 (respectively P2).
Therefore, there is a map from the leaf space of F1 + F2 to the product of the
leaf spaces of P1 and P2. The image R of this map gives a relation between the leaf
space of P1 and the leaf space of P2. Additionally, the projection of R to either
factor of the product is surjective. Because the ï¬bers of J1, J2 are connected, it
follows that R is the graph of a bijection.

9.5
Representation Equivalence
The Morita equivalence of Poisson manifolds provides a classical analogue to the
Morita equivalence of algebras. Let A1, A2 be algebras over a ï¬eld K. Deï¬ne an
(A1, A2)-bimodule E to be an abelian group E with a left action of A1 and a right
action of A2 such that for a1 âˆˆA1, a2 âˆˆA2, e âˆˆE
(a1e)a2 = a1(ea2) .
So we have injective maps
A1
âˆ’â†’
EndK(E)
Aopp
2
âˆ’â†’
EndK(E) .

56
9
DUAL PAIRS IN POISSON GEOMETRY
where Aopp
2
denotes A2 acting on the left by inverses. A Morita equivalence from
A1 to A2 is an (A1, A2)-bimodule E such that A1 and A2 are mutual commutants
in EndK(E). Morita introduced this as a weak equivalence between algebras, and he
showed that it implies that A1-modules and A2-modules are equivalent categories.
Xu [176, 177] showed that we can imitate this construction for symplectic real-
izations of Poisson manifolds. In particular, if P1, P2 are Poisson manifolds, then we
say that they are representation equivalent if the category of complete Poisson
maps to P1 is equivalent to the category of complete Poisson maps to P2. Xu then
proved the following theorem:
Theorem 9.3 (Xu [176, 177]) If two Poisson manifolds are Morita equivalent,
then they are representation equivalent.
For a survey of Xuâ€™s work and Morita equivalence in general, see the article
by Meyer [118]. For a survey of the relation between Poisson geometry and von
Neumann algebras, see the article by Shlyakhtenko [151].
9.6

deï¬nition of Morita equivalence between Poisson manifolds is explained by the
following property for the case where P1 and P2 are symplectic.

Part V
Generalized Functions
11
Group Algebras
Multiplication on a (locally compact) group G can be coded into a coproduct struc-
ture on the algebra C(G) of continuous real functions on G, making it into a com-
mutative Hopf algebra. Conversely, the algebra C(G) determines the multiplication
on G. Noncommutative analogues of C(G) are studied as if they were algebras of
functions on so-called quantum groups.
11.1
Hopf Algebras

11.2
Commutative and Noncommutative Hopf Algebras
When A = C(G) is the algebra of continuous real functions on a locally compact
topological group G, the (pointwise) multiplication of functions extends to a prod-
uct on a topological completion â€œ âŠ—â€ of the standard algebraic tensor product for
which C(G Ã— G) â‰ƒC(G)â€œ âŠ—â€C(G) (see [159]). The algebra C(G) is a commuta-
tive Hopf algebra (commutativity here refers to the ï¬rst multiplication) where
1. the multiplication is pointwise multiplication of functions,
2. the comultiplication is the pull-back mâˆ—of the multiplication on G,
3. the identity is the function identically equal to 1, or, equivalently, the homo-
morphism C â†’C(G), c 7â†’Ï• â‰¡c,
4. the coidentity is given by evaluation at the identity of G, and
5. the antipode is the pull-back by the inversion map on G.
Exercise
33
Show that the associativity of group multiplication on G translates to coasso-
ciativity on C(G).
Commutative Hopf algebras are closely related to groups: if A = C(X) is the
set of continuous functions on a locally compact Hausdorï¬€space X, then a Hopf
algebra structure on A (with C(X Ã— X) playing the role of A âŠ—A) deï¬nes a (not
necessarily commutative) multiplication on X which can be shown to satisfy the
group axioms.
A noncommutative Hopf algebra is thus to be thought of as â€œthe algebra
of functions on a quantum groupâ€. There is no universally accepted deï¬nition
of a quantum group. Many people restrict the name to the objects obtained by
deforming a Hopf algebra of functions on a Lie group.
Between commutative and noncommutative Hopf algebras lies the category of
Poisson Hopf algebras. A Poisson Hopf algebra A is a commutative Hopf algebra
equipped with a bracket operation making A into a Poisson algebra.
We then
require the comultiplication and co-unit to be Poisson algebra homomorphisms,
while the antipode is an anti-homomorphism. When A = Câˆ(P) for some Poisson
manifold P, the comultiplication gives P the structure of a Poisson Lie group;
i.e. the multiplication map P Ã— P â†’P is a Poisson map. Poisson Lie groups can
be regarded as the transitional objects between groups and quantum groups, or as
classical limits of quantum groups. A comprehensive reference on quantum groups
and Poisson Lie groups is [25].

11.4
Convolution of Functions
If we choose a reference Borel measure Î» on G, we can identify locally integrable
functions Ï• on G with measures by Ï• 7â†’Ï•Î». The map from compactly supported

they will map to the same measure). In any case, we can use this rough identiï¬cation
together with convolution of measures to describe a new product on functions on

Before we can do this, we need to make a digression through measures on groups.
We deï¬ne a measure Î» to be quasi-invariant if, for each g âˆˆG, the measure (â„“g)âˆ—Î»
induced by left translation is absolutely continuous with respect to Î»; in other words,
there is a locally integrable function Ï• such that (â„“g)âˆ—Î» = Ï•Î». We deï¬ne Î» to be
left-invariant if (â„“g)âˆ—Î» = Î» for all g âˆˆG.
Theorem 11.1 If G is locally compact, then there exists a left-invariant measure
which is unique up to multiplication by positive scalars.
Such a measure is called a Haar measure.

11.5

In Sections 11.2 and 11.3, we realized both C(G) and its dual Câ€²(G) as Hopf
algebras, with products and coproducts naturally induced from the group structure
of G.
There is a smooth version of this construction. If G is a Lie group, then D(G) =
Câˆ(G) is a Hopf algebra. The product is pointwise multiplication of functions,
while the coproduct is again the pull-back of group multiplication. The â€œtensorâ€
here needs to be a smooth kind of completion, so that Câˆ(G)â€œ âŠ—â€Câˆ(G) becomes
Câˆ(G Ã— G).
The dual space Dâ€²(G) of compactly supported distributions [148] is called the
distribution group algebra of G. The space Dâ€²(G) is larger than the measure
group algebra: an example of a distribution that is not a measure is evaluation
of a second derivative at a given point. As in the case of Câ€²(G), we can deï¬ne a
product (convolution) and coproduct (push-forward of the diagonal map) on Dâ€²(G)
to provide a Hopf algebra structure.

12
Densities
As we have seen, group algebras, measure group algebras and distribution group
algebras encode much, if not all, of the structure of the underlying group. There are
counterparts of these algebras for the case of manifolds, as algebras of â€œgeneralized
functionsâ€.
12.1
Densities
To construct spaces of distributions which behave as generalized functions, rather
than measures, we need the notion of density on a manifold.

A subset H of a groupoid G over X is called a subgroupoid if it is closed under
multiplication (when deï¬ned) and inversion. Note that
h âˆˆH â‡’hâˆ’1 âˆˆH â‡’both Îµ(Î±(h)) âˆˆH and Îµ(Î²(h)) âˆˆH .
Therefore, the subgroupoid H is a groupoid over Î±(H) = Î²(H), which may or may
not be all of X. When Î±(H) = Î²(H) = X, H is called a wide subgroupoid.
Examples.
1. If G = X is the trivial groupoid, then any subset of G is a subgroupoid, and
the only wide subgroupoid is G itself.
2. If X is a one point set, so that G is a group, then the nonempty subgroupoids
are the subgroups of G, but the empty set is also a subgroupoid of G.
3. If G = X Ã— X is the pair groupoid, then a subgroupoid H is a relation on X
which is symmetric and transitive. A wide subgroupoid H is an equivalence
relation. In general, H is an equivalence relation on the set Î±(H) = Î²(H) âŠ†
X.
â™¦
Given two groupoids G1 and G2 over sets X1 and X2 respectively, a morphism
of groupoids is a pair of maps G1 â†’G2 and X1 â†’X2 which commute with all
the structural functions of G1 and G2. We depict a morphism by the following
diagram.

from G to the pair groupoid over X. Its image is a wide subgroupoid of X Ã— X,
and hence deï¬nes an equivalence relation on X. The equivalence classes are called
the orbits of G in X. In category language, the orbits are the isomorphism classes
of the objects of the category. We can also think of a groupoid as an equivalence
relation where two elements might be equivalent in diï¬€erent ways, parametrized by
the kernel of (Î±, Î²). The groupoid further indicates the structure of the set of all
ways in which two elements are equivalent.
Inside the groupoid X Ã—X there is a diagonal subgroupoid âˆ†= {(x, x) | x âˆˆ
X}. We call (Î±, Î²)âˆ’1(âˆ†) the isotropy subgroupoid of G.

If x, y âˆˆX are in the same orbit, then any element g of
Gx,y := (Î±, Î²)âˆ’1(x, y) = {g âˆˆG | Î±(g) = x and Î²(g) = y}
induces an isomorphism h 7â†’gâˆ’1hg from Gx to Gy. On the other hand, the groups
Gx and Gy have natural commuting, free transitive actions on Gx,y, by left and
right multiplication, respectively. Consequently, Gx,y is isomorphic (as a set) to Gx
(and to Gy), but not in a natural way.
A groupoid is called transitive if it has just one orbit. The transitive groupoids
are the building blocks of groupoids, in the following sense. There is a natural
decomposition of the base space of a general groupoid into orbits. Over each orbit
there is a transitive groupoid, and the disjoint union of these transitive groupoids
is the original groupoid.
Historical Remark.
Brandt [17] discovered groupoids while studying quadratic
forms over the integers. Groupoids also appeared in Galois theory in the description
of relations between subï¬elds of a ï¬eld K via morphisms of K [108]. The isotropy
groups of the constructed groupoid turn out to be the Galois groups. Groupoids
occur also as generalizations of equivalence relations in the work of Grothendieck
on moduli spaces [75] and in the work of Mackey on ergodic theory [113]. For recent
applications in these two areas, see Keel and Mori [94] and Connes [32].

1. Let X be a topological space and let G = Î (X) be the collection of homotopy

if Î³ is a path from x to y, the target is Î±(x, [Î³], y) = x and the source is
Î²(x, [Î³], y) = y.) The groupoid Î (X) is called the fundamental groupoid
of X. The orbits of Î (X) are just the path components of X.
See Brownâ€™s

There are several advantages of the fundamental groupoid over the fundamen-
tal group. First notice that the fundamental group sits within the fundamen-
tal groupoid as the isotropy subgroup over a single point. The fundamental
groupoid does not require a choice of base point and is better suited to study
spaces that are not path connected. Additionally, many of the algebraic prop-
erties of the fundamental group generalize to the fundamental groupoid, as
illustrated in the following exercise.

1. Topological groupoids:

2. Measurable groupoids:

The groupoid G and the

4. Bundles of groups:
A groupoid for which Î± = Î² is called a bundle of
groups. This is not necessarily a trivial bundle, or even a locally trivial bundle
in the topological case, as the ï¬bers need not be isomorphic as groups or as
topological spaces. The orbits are the individual points of the base space, and
the isotropy subgroupoids are the ï¬ber groups of the bundle.
13.5
The Holonomy Groupoid of a Foliation
Let X be a (Hausdorï¬€) manifold. Let F âŠ†TX be an integrable subbundle, and
F the corresponding foliation (F is the decomposition of X into maximal integral
manifolds called leaves).
The notion of holonomy can be described as follows.
An F-path is a path in X whose tangent vectors lie within F.
Suppose that
Î³ : [0, 1] â†’O is an F-path along a leaf O. Let NÎ³(0) and NÎ³(1) be cross-sections
for the spaces of leaves near Î³(0) and Î³(1), respectively, i.e. they are two small
transversal manifolds to the foliation at the end points of Î³. There is an F-path
near Î³ from each point near Î³(0) in NÎ³(0) to a uniquely determined point in NÎ³(1).
This deï¬nes a local diï¬€eomorphism between the two leaf spaces. The holonomy
of Î³ is deï¬ned to be the germ, or direct limit, of such diï¬€eomorphisms, between the
local leaf spaces NÎ³(0) and NÎ³(1).
The notion of holonomy allows us to deï¬ne an equivalence relation on the set
of F-paths from x to y in X. Let [Î³]H denote the equivalence class of Î³ under the
relation that two paths are equivalent if they have the same holonomy.
5Throughout these notes, a manifold is assumed to be Hausdorï¬€, unless it is a groupoid.

The holonomy groupoid [32], also called the graph of the foliation [175], is
H(F) = {(x, [Î³]H, y) | x, y âˆˆX, Î³ is an F-path from x to y} .
Given a foliation F, there are two other related groupoids obtained by changing
the equivalence relation on paths:
1. The F-pair groupoid â€“ This groupoid is the equivalence relation for which
the equivalence classes are the leaves of F, i.e. we consider any two F-paths
between x, y âˆˆO to be equivalent.
2. The F-fundamental groupoid â€“ For this groupoid, two F-paths between
x, y are equivalent if and only if they are F-homotopic, that is, homotopic
within the set of all F-paths. Let [Î³]F denote the equivalence class of Î³ under
F-homotopy. The set of this groupoid is
Î (F) = {(x, [Î³]F , y) | x, y âˆˆX, Î³ is an F-path from x to y} .

groupoid and the F-fundamental groupoid:

Groupoid algebras include matrix algebras, algebras of functions, and group alge-
bras. We refer the reader to [101, 135, 143] for extensive discussion of groupoid
algebras as sources of noncommutative algebras in physics and mathematics.

14.4
Groupoid Actions
A groupoid G over X â‰ƒG(0) may act on sets M
Âµâ†’X that map to X. Let G âˆ—M
be the space
G âˆ—M := {(g, m) âˆˆG Ã— M | Î²(g) = Âµ(m)} .
A (left) groupoid action of G on M is deï¬ned to be a map G âˆ—M â†’M, taking
the pair (g, m) to g Â· m, with the properties:
1. Âµ(g Â· m) = Î±(g),
2. (gh) Â· m = g Â· (h Â· m),
3. (ÎµÂµ(m)) Â· m = m.
The map Âµ : M â†’X is sometimes called the moment map, by analogy with
symplectic geometry.
Remark.
The terms â€œmoment mapâ€ and â€œmomentum mapâ€ are usually used
interchangeably in the literature, with diï¬€erent authors preferring each of these
two translations of Souriauâ€™s [153] French term, â€œmomentâ€. By contrast, in these
notes, we have used the terms in diï¬€erent ways. Here, a â€œmomentum mapâ€ is a
Poisson map J : M â†’gâˆ—to a Lie-Poisson manifold gâˆ—, generating a hamiltonian
action of an underlying Lie group G on M. On the other hand, a â€œmoment mapâ€
is a map Âµ : M â†’X to the base X of a groupoid G which is acting on M.
â™¦
Example.
A groupoid G over X acts on G by left multiplication with moment
map Î± and on X with moment map the identity.
â™¦

We can think of a representation of a groupoid as a collection of representations
of the isotropy subgroups together with ways of identifying these representations
using diï¬€erent â€œarrowsâ€ in X.
Example.
If X is a topological space, and G = Î (X) is the fundamental
groupoid, then a representation of Î (X) on a vector bundle V would be a ï¬‚at
connection of V . By ï¬‚at connection, we do not yet mean a diï¬€erential-geometric
notion, but rather a topological one, namely that parallel transport only depends
on the homotopy class of the base path.
To see the ï¬‚at connection, recall that Î (X) is the collection of homotopy classes
of paths in X.
A representation of Î (X) determines precisely how to parallel
translate along paths to deï¬ne a connection.
With this ï¬‚at connection, we can look at the isotropy subgroup of loops based
at a point. The fundamental group of X acts on each ï¬ber in the usual sense, and
we thus see that the representation of the fundamental groupoid on V includes the
action of the fundamental group on a ï¬ber of V .
For applications to the moduli spaces used in topological quantum ï¬eld theory,
see [77].

As with groups, the notion of groupoid representation can be formalized in
terms of the following deï¬nition. The general linear groupoid of a vector bundle

GL(V ) = {(x, â„“, y) | x, y âˆˆX, â„“: Âµâˆ’1(y) â†’Âµâˆ’1(x) is a linear isomorphism} .
The isotropy subgroup over any point is the general linear group of the correspond-
ing ï¬ber of V . A representation of G in V is then a groupoid homomorphism
from G to GL(V ), covering the identity map on X.

Extended groupoid algebras encompass bisections and sections of the normal bundle

elements and Lie algebra elements.

Lie Algebroids
Lie algebroids are the inï¬nitesimal versions of Lie groupoids.
16.1
Deï¬nitions
A Lie algebroid over a manifold X is a (real) vector bundle E over X together
with a bundle map Ï : E â†’TX and a (real) Lie algebra structure [Â·, Â·]E on Î“(E)
such that:
1. The induced map Î“(Ï) : Î“(E) â†’Ï‡(X) is a Lie algebra homomorphism.
2. For any f âˆˆCâˆ(X) and v, w âˆˆÎ“(E), the following Leibniz identity holds
[v, fw]E = f[v, w]E + (Ï(v) Â· f)w .
Remarks.
â€¢ The map Ï is called the anchor of the Lie algebroid. By an abuse of notation,
the map Î“(Ï) may be denoted simply by Ï and also called the anchor.
â€¢ For each v âˆˆÎ“(E), we deï¬ne E-Lie derivative operations on both Î“(E) and
Câˆ(X) by
Lvw = [v, w]E ,
Lvf = Ï(v) Â· f .
We can then view the Leibniz identity as a derivation rule
Lv(fw) = f (Lvw) + (Lvf) w .
â™¦
When (E, Ï, [Â·, Â·]E) is a Lie algebroid over X, the kernel of Ï is called the
isotropy. Each ï¬ber of ker Ï is a Lie algebra, analogous to the isotropy subgroups
of groupoids. To see this, let v and w âˆˆÎ“(E) be such that Ï(v) and Ï(w) both
vanish at a given point x âˆˆX. Then, for any function f âˆˆCâˆ(X),
[v, fw]E(x) = f(x)[v, w]E(x) .
So there is a well-deï¬ned bracket operation on the vectors in any ï¬ber of ker Ï, and
ker Ï is a ï¬eld of Lie algebras. These form a bundle when Ï has constant rank.
On the other hand, the image of Ï is an integrable distribution analogous
to the image of eÎ  for Poisson manifolds. Therefore, X can be decomposed into
submanifolds, called orbits of the Lie algebroid, whose tangent spaces are the image
of Ï. There are are various proofs of this: one uses the corresponding (local) Lie
groupoid, another uses a kind of splitting theorem, and a third proof involves a more
general approach to integrating singular distributions. The articles of Dazord [37,
38] discuss this and related issues.

16.2
First Examples of Lie Algebroids
1. A (ï¬nite dimensional real) Lie algebra is a Lie algebroid over a one-point
space.
2. A bundle of Lie algebras over a manifold X (as in Section 16.3) is a Lie
algebroid over X, with Ï â‰¡0. Conversely, if E is any Lie algebroid with
Ï â‰¡0, the Leibniz identity says that the bracket in Î“(E) is a bilinear map of
Câˆ(X)-modules and not simply of R-modules, and hence that each ï¬ber is a
Lie algebra. (Such an E is all isotropy.)
3. We saw in Section 15.5 that the normal bundle E along the identity section of
a Lie groupoid G over X carries a bracket operation and anchor Ï : E â†’TX
satisfying the Lie algebroid conditions. This is called the Lie algebroid of
the Lie groupoid G. The isotropy algebras of this Lie algebroid are the Lie
algebras of the isotropy groups of G. The orbits are the connected components
of the G-orbits.
As for the case of Lie groups and Lie algebras, it is natural to pose the
integrability problem (see also Sections 16.3 and 16.4):
â€¢ When is a given Lie algebroid the Lie algebroid of a Lie groupoid?
â€¢ If the Lie algebroid does come from a Lie groupoid, is the Lie groupoid
unique?
4. The tangent bundle TX of a manifold X, with Ï the identity map, is a Lie
algebroid over X. We can see it the Lie algebroid of the Lie groupoid X Ã— X,
or of the fundamental groupoid Î (X), or of yet other possibilities; near the
identity section, Î (X) looks like X Ã— X.
Generally, we can say that a Lie algebroid determines and is determined by
a neighborhood of the identity section in the groupoid, just as a Lie algebra
determines and is determined by a neighborhood of the identity element in
the corresponding Lie group.

Historical Remark.
Already in 1963, Rinehart [145] noted that, if a Lie algebra
Î“ over a ï¬eld k is a module over a commutative k-algebra C, and if there is a
homomorphism Ï from Î“ into the derivations of C, then there is a semidirect product
Lie bracket on the sum Î“ âŠ•C deï¬ned by the formula
[(v, g), (w, h)] = ([v, w], Ï(v) Â· h âˆ’Ï(w) Â· g) .
Furthermore, this bracket satisï¬es the Leibniz identity:
[(v, g), f(w, h)] = f[(v, g), (w, h)] + (Ï(v) Â· f)(w, h)
for f âˆˆC .
In the special case where C = Câˆ(X), the Câˆ(X)-module Î“, if projective, is
the space of sections of some vector bundle E over X. The homomorphism Ï and
the Leibniz identity imply that Ï is induced by a bundle map Ï : E â†’TX. The
Leibniz identity for Î“(E)âŠ•Câˆ(X) also encodes the Leibniz identity for the bracket
on Î“(E) alone.
In 1967, Pradines [139] coined the term â€œLie algebroidâ€ and proved that every
Lie algebroid comes from a (local) Lie groupoid. He asserted that the local condition
was not needed, but this was later shown by Almeida and Molino [4] to be false.
(See Section 16.4.)
Rinehart [145] proved (in a more algebraic setting) an analogue of the PoincarÂ´e-
Birkhoï¬€-Witt theorem for Lie algebroids. He showed that there is a linear iso-
morphism between the graded version of a universal object for the actions of
Î“(E) âŠ•Câˆ(X) on vector bundles V over X, and the polynomials on the dual
of the Lie algebroid E. As a result, the dual bundle of a Lie algebroid carries a
Poisson structure. This Poisson structure is described abstractly in [34] as the base
of the cotangent groupoid T âˆ—G of a Lie groupoid G; it is described more explicitly
in [35]. (See Section 16.5.)

16.3
Bundles of Lie Algebras
For a ï¬rst look at the integrability problem, we examine Lie algebroids for which
the anchor map is zero.
A bundle of Lie groups is a bundle of groups (see Section 13.4) for which each
ï¬ber is a Lie group. Bundles of Lie algebras are vector bundles for which each ï¬ber
has a Lie algebra structure which varies continuously (or smoothly). Every bundle
of Lie groups deï¬nes a bundle of Lie algebras: the Lie algebras of the individual
ï¬bers. More problematic is the question of whether we can integrate a bundle of
Lie algebras to get a bundle of Lie groups.
Theorem 16.1 (Douady-Lazard [48]) Every bundle of Lie algebras can be inte-
grated to a (not necessarily Hausdorï¬€) bundle of Lie groups. (Fibers and base are
Hausdorï¬€, but the bundle itself might not be.)

16.6
Complex Lie Algebroids
It can be interesting to work over C even if X is a real manifold.
To deï¬ne a
complex Lie algebroid geometrically, take a complex vector bundle E over X
and a complex bundle map Ï : E â†’TCX to the complexiï¬ed tangent bundle.7 The
immediate generalization of our deï¬nition in Section 16.1 amounts to imposing that
the space of sections of E be a complex Lie algebra satisfying the (complex versions
of the) two axioms.
Example.
Let X be a (real) manifold with an almost complex structure J :
TX â†’TX, i.e. J is a bundle map such that J2 = âˆ’id. The graph of âˆ’iJ in
TCX = TX âŠ•iTX is the sub-bundle
E = {v âˆ’iJ(v)| v âˆˆTX} âŠ†TCX .
The bracket operation on TX extends by linearity to a bracket on TCX. To endow
E with a Lie algebroid structure, we need the sections of E to be closed under that
bracket:
[Î“(E), Î“(E)] âŠ†Î“(E) .
This holds if and only if J is an integrable structure. That is, by the Newlander-
Nirenberg theorem [131], we have a complex Lie algebroid structure on E if and
only if J comes from a complex structure on X.
A complex structure on a
7Algebraically, we have changed our â€œground ringâ€ from Câˆ(X) to Câˆ(X; C).

16.6
Complex Lie Algebroids
121
manifold is, in this way, a typical example of a complex Lie algebroid. The natural
questions arise: When does such a Lie algebroid come from a complex Lie groupoid?
What is a complex Lie groupoid?
â™¦
Example.
Let X be a manifold of dimension 2n âˆ’1. Suppose that F âŠ†TX is a
codimension-1 sub-bundle with an almost complex structure J : F â†’F (J is linear
and J2 = âˆ’id). As before, deï¬ne a sub-bundle E of the complexiï¬ed F to be the
graph of âˆ’iJ
E = {v âˆ’iJ(v)| v âˆˆF} âŠ‚FC = F âŠ•iF .
If Î“(E) is closed under the bracket operation, i.e. if E is a Lie algebroid over X,
then (F, J) is called a Cauchy-Riemann structure or CR-structure on X.
To explain the motivation behind this construction, we consider the special
situation when X2nâˆ’1 âŠ‚Y 2n is a real submanifold of a complex n-manifold Y .
At a point x âˆˆX âŠ‚Y , the tangent space TxY is a vector space over C.
We
denote by JY the complex multiplication by i in this space. Because X has odd
real dimension, TxX cannot be equal to JY (TxX) as subspaces of TxY , and thus
the intersection Fx := TxX âˆ©JY (TxX) must have codimension 1 in TxX. Then F
is the maximal complex sub-bundle of TX.
Functions on a CR-manifold annihilated by the sections of E are called CR-
functions. In the case where X is a hypersurface in a complex manifold Y , they
include (and sometimes coincide with) the restrictions to X of holomorphic func-
tions on one side of X in Y .
This construction opens several questions, including:
â€¢ What is the Lie groupoid in this case? However, at this point it is not clear
what it means to integrate a complex Lie algebroid.
â€¢ What does the analytic theory of complex Lie algebroids look like? It seems
to be at least as complicated as that of CR-structures, which is already very
delicate [87].
â€¢ The cohomology theory of Lie algebroids can be applied to complex Lie al-
gebroids. In the examples above, we recover the usual âˆ‚cohomology and
boundary âˆ‚cohomology on complex and CR-manifolds, respectively. What
does complex Lie algebroid cohomology look like in more general cases?
â™¦
Remark.
When X is a complex manifold, it is tempting to impose the Lie alge-
broid axioms on the space of holomorphic sections of a holomorphic vector bundle
E. This idea fails in general, for the following reason. In the real case, sections of
E always exist. On the other hand, the only holomorphic functions on a compact
complex manifold are the constant functions. Similarly, it is possible that there
are no non-zero holomorphic sections for a complex vector bundle. It is therefore
more appropriate to look instead at the sheaf of local sections. Atiyahâ€™s (see [9] and
Section 17.1) study of the obstructions to the existence of holomorphic connections
on principal GL(n; C)-bundles over complex manifolds used this approach to the
â€œAtiyah algebroidâ€.

Examples of Lie Algebroids
Lie algebroids with surjective anchor map are called transitive Lie algebroids,
Atiyah algebras, or Atiyah sequences because of Atiyahâ€™s work mentioned
below. When a corresponding groupoid exists, it will be (locally) transitive, in the
sense that its orbits are open.
17.1

Remarks.
1. When Y = âˆ‚X, A is the Lie algebroid of a groupoid over X, namely the
groupoid built from the pair groupoid of X \ Y together with the gauge
groupoid of the normal bundle of Y in X.
Exercise
68
What if Y is not the boundary of X?
2. In general, if the hypersurface Y is not the boundary of X, then the Y -tangent
bundle A might be not isomorphic to the tangent bundle TX.
For example, let X be a circle and let Y be one point. Then the Y -tangent
bundle is a MÂ¨obius band rather than the trivial bundle. A similar construction
works when X is a 2-torus and Y is a single homologically nontrivial closed
curve.
Notice that, if Y is two points on a circle X, then the Y -tangent bundle is
again the trivial bundle.
It would be interesting to understand how much of the structure of the Y -
tangent bundle is determined by the cohomology class dual to Y (and the
original tangent bundle).

The theorem is saying that:
1. Every k-cocycle is cohomologous to a skew-symmetric cocycle.
2. Every skew-symmetric cocycle is given by a k-vector ï¬eld.
3. A k-vector ï¬eld is a coboundary only if it is zero.

classiï¬cation of formal deformations of the standard associative multiplication on

these issues from a â€œpre-Kontsevichâ€ viewpoint in the remainder of these notes.

Historical Remarks.
The program of quantizing a symplectic manifold M with a
âˆ—-product, that is an associative multiplication on formal power series Câˆ(M)[[â„]],
was ï¬rst set out by Bayen, Flato, Fronsdal, Lichnerowicz and Sternheimer in the
70â€™s [12].
In 1983 [43], De Wilde and Lecomte showed that every symplectic manifold
admits a formal deformation quantization. Their proof involved rather complicated
calculations which made the result look quite technical.
Some later versions of the existence proof relied on patching together local Weyl
algebras with nonlinear coordinate changes.
In [92], Karasev and Maslov gave
further details of a proof, whose ï¬rst outline was sketched in [91], which reduces
the patching to standard sheaf-theoretic ideas.
Another proof of the existence of deformation quantization which uses patching
ideas was given by Omori, Maeda and Yoshioka [133]. Although their proof still

19.5
Deformations of the Product of Functions
147
involved substantial computations, it used a fundamental idea which is also basic
in the proof of Fedosov (who discovered it independently). Each tangent space of
a Poisson manifold M can be viewed as an aï¬ƒne space with a constant Poisson
structure, so it carries a natural Moyal-Weyl quantization (see Section 20.1). In
this way, the tangent bundle TM becomes a Poisson manifold with the ï¬brewise
Poisson bracket, and with a ï¬brewise quantization. To quantize M itself, we may
try to identify a subalgebra of the quantized algebra Câˆ(TM)[[â„]] with the vector
space Câˆ(M)[[â„]] in such a way that the induced multiplication on Câˆ(M)[[â„]]
gives a deformation quantization of M.
Such an identiï¬cation is called a Weyl
structure in [133].
In Chapter 21 we will discuss Fedosovâ€™s proof of existence of deformation quan-
tization on symplectic manifolds.
For the history of these developments, see [14, 60].

20.3
Aï¬ƒne Invariance of the Weyl Product
The Weyl product on a Poisson vector space (E, Î ) is invariant under aï¬ƒne Poisson
maps, i.e. if A : E â†’E is an aï¬ƒne symplectic map, then the induced pull-back
map
Aâˆ—: Câˆ(E)[[â„]] âˆ’â†’Câˆ(E)[[â„]]
is an algebra automorphism for the Weyl product.
By aï¬ƒne invariance, the Weyl product (on the Weyl algebra or any of the
other related spaces of functions mentioned at the end of the previous section)
passes to any Poisson manifold locally modeled on E, as long as we only allow
aï¬ƒne coordinate changes.
This condition on E amounts to the existence of a
ï¬‚at connection without torsion, for which parallel transport preserves the Poisson
structure.
The inï¬nitesimal counterpart of aï¬ƒne invariance is that, for every polynomial
function k on E of degree less than or equal to 2,
{f â‹†â„g, k} = f â‹†â„{g, k} + {f, k} â‹†â„g .
In words, {Â·, k} is a derivation not just of the pointwise product (Leibniz identity)
and of the Poisson bracket (Jacobi identity), but of the whole â‹†-product.
Remark.
Diracâ€™s [44] quantum Poisson bracket
[f, g]â‹†:= f â‹†â„g âˆ’g â‹†â„f
iâ„
satisï¬es the derivation law
[f â‹†â„g, k]â‹†= f â‹†â„[g, k]â‹†+ [f, k]â‹†â‹†â„g
just as a consequence of associativity. The similar property for {Â·, k} is explained
by the fact that, for a polynomial k on E of degree â‰¤2, we have [Â·, k]â‹†= {Â·, k}.
In particular, for k1 and k2 polynomials of degree â‰¤2, we have [k1, k2]â‹†=
{k1, k2}, which shows that polynomials of degree â‰¤2 form a Lie algebra.

A theorem of E. Borel states that every formal power series is the Taylor ex-
pansion of some function. This implies that the space F(E) of formal power series
on the vector space E is isomorphic to Câˆ(E) modulo the functions which vanish
to inï¬nite order at 0.

21
Deformation Quantization
On a general Poisson manifold, if the rank of the Poisson tensor Î  is constant, then
by a theorem of Lie the Poisson manifold is locally isomorphic to a vector space
with constant Poisson structure (see Section 3.4). Such Poisson manifolds, which
are called regular, are always locally deformation quantizable using the Moyal-
Weyl product in canonical coordinates; the problem is to patch together the local
deformations to produce a global â‹†-product.
21.1
Fedosovâ€™s Connection
There is one case in which the patching together of local quantizations is easy.
Since the Moyal-Weyl product on a vector space V with constant Poisson structure
is invariant under all the aï¬ƒne automorphisms of V , we can construct a global
quantization of any Poisson manifold (M, Î ) covered by canonical coordinate sys-
tems in the general case for which the transition maps are aï¬ƒne. Such a covering
exists when M admits a ï¬‚at torsionless linear connection for which the covariant
derivative of Î  is zero.
Fedosov overcomes the diï¬ƒculty of patching together local Weyl structures by
making the canonical coordinate neighborhoods â€œinï¬nitely smallâ€. To understand
his idea, we should ï¬rst think of elements of the deformed algebra Câˆ(TM)[[â„]] as
sections of the bundle WTM over M whose ï¬ber at x âˆˆM is WTxM.
Of course we are most interested in dealing with the case where (M, Î ) does
not admit a ï¬‚at Poisson connection, and this is where the most interesting part of
Fedosovâ€™s proof comes in. He shows (in other terms) that the tangent bundle of
every symplectic (or regular Poisson) manifold does admit a ï¬‚at Poisson connection,
if one gives the appropriate extended meaning to that concept, namely admitting
â€œnonlinear quantum mapsâ€ as the structure group.
Fedosovâ€™s connection is constructed on the bundle WTM of Weyl algebras.
The â€œstructure Lie algebraâ€ of this connection, in which the connection forms take
values, is WR2n acting on itself by the adjoint representation of its Lie algebra
structure. Since the full Weyl algebra is used, and not just the quadratic functions
which generate linear symplectic transformations, the structure group allows non-
linear transformations of the (quantized) tangent spaces. Since linear generating
functions are included, the structure group even allows translations.
In fact (this idea was also used in [133]), it is not the full Weyl algebra of
R2n which serves as the typical ï¬ber, but only the formal Weyl algebra F(WR2n),
consisting of formal Taylor expansions at the origin. Geometrically, one can think
of this step as the replacement of the (quantized) tangent bundle by a formal
neighborhood of the zero section
QTM.
Remark.
Since
QTM is an inï¬nitesimal neighborhood of the zero section, parallel
transport does not go anywhere. This step may hence appear to be inconsistent
with the inclusion of translations in the structure group, since these do not leave
the origin ï¬xed. In fact, the eï¬€ect is to force us to forget the group and to work only
with the structure Lie algebra. A beneï¬cial, and somewhat surprising, result of this
eï¬€ect is that a parallel section with respect to a ï¬‚at connection is not determined by
its value at a single point. This situation is very close to that in formal diï¬€erential
geometry, where the bundle of inï¬nite jets of functions on a manifold M has a ï¬‚at

connection whose sections are the lifts of functions on M. (See [160, Section 1] for
a nice exposition with references.)

Fedosov uses an iterative method for â€œï¬‚atteningâ€ a connection which is similar
to that used in many diï¬€erential geometric problems. (See [119] for an example,
and [147] for a recent survey.) Over the domain of a local trivialization of a principal

Up to now, we have essentially been following Newtonâ€™s method for solving

to Nash and Moser. (See [155, Section III.6] for an exposition of this method with
original references.) Since the linear diï¬€erential equation we are trying to solve

to take care of the neglected quadratic term âˆ’1

geometric applications mentioned above, the full story involves elliptic diï¬€erential
operators, Sobolev spaces, and so on, but in the case at hand, it turns out that the
â€œHodge theoryâ€ is purely algebraic and quite trivial.

21.3
A Derivation and Filtration of the Weyl Algebra
The coeï¬ƒcients of the connection forms which we are using are sections of the bun-
dle FW(TM). Rather than measuring the size of these forms by the usual Sobolev
norms involving derivatives, we shall use a pointwise algebraic measurement.
In the formal Weyl algebra FW(V ) of a Poisson vector space V , we assign
degree 2 to the variable â„and degree 1 to each linear function on V . We denote
by FWr(V ) the ideal generated by the monomials of degree r. Because the kth
term in the expansion of the â‹†-product involves 2k derivatives and multiplication
by â„k, we obtain a ï¬ltration of the algebra FW(V ). We will also occasionally use
the classical grading, compatible with the commutative multiplication but not with
the â‹†-product, which assigns degree 0 to â„and 1 to each linear function on V .
The Lie algebra structure which we use for the formal Weyl algebra is the
quantum Poisson bracket of Dirac [44] deï¬ned in Section 20.3. The factor (1/iâ„)
makes the quantum bracket reduce to the classical one (rather than to zero) when
â„â†’0. In addition, the quantum and classical brackets are equal when one of the
entries contains only terms linear or quadratic in the variable on V , and they share
the property

so that the adjoint action of any element of FW2(V ) preserves the ï¬ltration.

diï¬€erential forms on the classical space V , in which case we generally use the classi-
cal grading. In this way, W(V ) inherits the exterior derivative operator, which we
denote by Î´. Remarkably, Î´ is also a derivation for the quantized algebra structure

Classiï¬cation of Deformation Quantizations
Fedosov [59] showed that his iterative construction of a connection on FW(TM)
can be modiï¬ed so that the curvature becomes P â„j âŠ—Ï‰j, for any sequence of closed
2-forms Ï‰j such that Ï‰0 is the original symplectic structure Ï‰. He also showed that
the isomorphism class of the resulting â‹†-product depends precisely on the sequence
of de Rham cohomology classes [Ï‰j] âˆˆH2(M, R) and in particular is independent
of the initial choice of connection Ï†0.
In summary, the relevant data for an equivalence class of deformation quanti-
zations on a manifold M is
Ï‰ , [Ï‰1] , [Ï‰2] , . . .
A representative of such an equivalence class is called a Fedosov quantization of
M.

162
21
DEFORMATION QUANTIZATION
This left open the question of whether every â‹†-product is isomorphic to one ob-
tained by Fedosovâ€™s construction. A positive answer to this question has been given
by Nest and Tsygan. Using a noncommutative version of Gelâ€™fand-Fuks cohomol-
ogy, they construct in [124] for each deformation quantization a characteristic class
in H2(M, R)[[â„]] with constant term Ï‰. In [125], they show that this class deter-
mines the â‹†-product up to isomorphism and that it agrees with Fedosovâ€™s curvature
for the â‹†-products constructed by his method. By Moserâ€™s classiï¬cation [121] of
nearby symplectic structures by their cohomology classes, the isomorphism classes
of â‹†-products on a symplectic manifold are thus in one-to-one correspondence with
isomorphism classes of formal deformations of the symplectic structure. Other ref-
erences concerning this classiï¬cation are Bertelson-Cahen-Gutt [15] Kontsevich [97],
and Weinstein-Xu [173].
One consequence of this classiï¬cation is that there is (up to isomorphism) a
unique deformation quantization whose characteristic class is independent of â„.
Although one might think that this special quantization is somehow the natural
one, there is considerable evidence that the others are important as well.
For
instance, [54] suggests that â‹†-products with nonconstant characteristic classes may
be related to geometric phases and deformations of symplectic forms which arise in
the analysis of coupled wave equations [107].
